### Post by TheFu on 2014-10-21
Please post output from:
```
df -h
du -sh  /

```
Use code tags please.
You'll need to find where the extra storage is hiding by going 1 level deeper and deeper - that GUI program should be easier for that.
However, if the extra data is hidden _under_ a mount point by other partitions mounted over it - you'll never find it.  You can google that for more details.

### Post by mcduck on 2014-10-21
That's interesting, your root partition is reported as 451GB in size and 358GB used, while the total space available, while Disk Usage Analyzer can only see 72GB of data on the filesystem.

Are you perhaps running some kind of automatic backups? If the filesystem where they are set to go isn't mounted while the backups happen, the files would end being written on your / partition, and then when the filesystem is mounted again, it would hide the files (but they'd still take space on /). And of course the input/output errors on /dev/sdb1 are worrying as well...

---

### Post by TheFu on 2014-10-21
Actually -  **du -sh /** was requested NOT **du -sH**. There is a difference.  code tags would be helpful too - we cannot easily copy/paste replies if images are used.  Adv Reply, then use the #.

The I/O errors for the NTFS storage is troubling ... but likely completely unrelated to the issue. Connect to a Windows box and have those disk tools fix it. Sooner than later is better to avoid more data loss. Backups. Hope you have some.

Since there isn't anything mounted outside /media/ in odd places, then all the storage use should easily be found using that GUI tool you have.

You aren't using LVM or encryption or anything else that could hide the used space.  I suspect there is a background task dumping files into your HOME in a slightly unknown ~/.* directory - perhaps btorrent or miro or a backup that expects to write to other storage, but that storage isn't connected.

### Post by mcduck on 2014-10-21
Where are the backups supposed to go? If they are going to external drive (or the sdb1), unmount that drive and then take a look at the mount point, if you see any files/directories inside it you should be able to just delete them. (Of course make sure your actual backup works well first. And in the future you might want to run the backups manually when you know the drive is connected rather than leaving it to run automatically)

---

### Post by TheFu on 2014-10-21
> **mcduck said:**
> Where are the backups supposed to go? If they are going to external drive (or the sdb1), unmount that drive and then take a look at the mount point, if you see any files/directories inside it you should be able to just delete them. (Of course make sure your actual backup works well first. And in the future you might want to run the backups manually when you know the drive is connected rather than leaving it to run automatically)

Or validate the backup storage is mounted before continuing.  
Backing up Linux stuff to either the same physical HDD or to NTFS storage is generally a bad idea. There are ways to make it fine, but most Linux backup tools don't address those details when writing to NTFS.

Of course, using network backup tools makes this go away too.

---

### Post by The Cog on 2014-10-22
du does its work by reading in all the directories and adding the file sizes. Unless you run it as root, it will very likely miss the contents of directories it cannot see into. 

Don't ever expect the usage to match exactly because du doesn't count the unused portions of part-filled disk sectors - sectors that contain a small file or the tail end of a file.
Also, I'm not sure if df counts the space reserved for root as used or not.

---

### Post by TheFu on 2014-10-22
> **savvy2 said:**
> Million thanks to everyone for your help, I finally identified the culprit it was failed backups for remote server..........its an experience

It has happened to me too .... and did until I added validation for mount points/network locations into my scripts.
Since I run mostly VMs, it is hard to backup a 20G VM that has 18G of data on it without external storage ... and not get an out-of-storage error fairly quickly. Trying to write 18G of backups to / just doesn't get far.  That is one of the reasons NOT to have a huge / partition - there are others.
